4 min
Craftsmen's Verification and the Limit of Certainty
Describes empirical verification and epistemic limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how craftsmen verify the theory through tangible transformation and why certainty is unattainable.

Core Idea
Craftsmen confirm the theory by congealing mercury with sulfur, but only strong supposition, not certainty, is possible.

The passage concludes by noting that alchemists can actually congeal mercury with sulfur, giving them a strong impression that natural processes mirror artificial ones. However, the author emphasizes that absolute certainty (القطع) is beyond anyone's reach.

Guided Path
1
Read the craftsmen's method
Read how craftsmen verify the claims: by a perceptible congealing of mercury with sulfur.
2
Understand the concept of غلبة الظن
This is a preponderance of opinion or strong probability, not certainty.
3
Reflect on the epistemic limitation
Why does the author say 'certainty is not within anyone's power'?
This shows humility about human knowledge of natural processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشهدت الأمارات بأن مادة الأجسام السبعة الزئبق والكبريت، واختلاف الأنواع والأصناف عائد إلى اختلاف صفاتهما واختلاطهما، وتأثر أحدهما عن الآخر، أما الأمارات فهي أنها سيما الرصاص تذوب إلى مثل الزئبق، والزئبق ينعقد برائحة الكبريت إلى مثل الرصاص والزئبق يتعلق بهذه الأجساد، ثم الزئبق مركب من مائية وكبريتية. وامتحان علم الصنعة أيضاً يشهد بذلك. واعتراض أبي البركات بأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لوجد كل من الزئبق والكبريت في معدن الآخر، وفي معادن هذه الأجسام مدفوع بأنه يجوز أن يكون عدم الوجدان لتغيرهما بالامتزاج، أو لعدم الاحساس بواسطة تصغر الأجزاء، وأما كيفية تكونها، فهي أنه إذا كان الزئبق والكبريت صافيين، وكان انطباخ أحدهما بالآخر تاماً، فإذا كان الكبريت مع بقائه أبيض غير محترق تكونت الفضة، وإن كان أحمر وفيه قوة صباغة لطيفة غير محرقة تكون الذهب وإن كانا نقيين وفي الكبريت قوة صباغة لكن وصل إليه قبل كمال النضج برد مجمد عاقد يكون الخارصيني، وإن كان الزئبق نقياً، والكبريت رديئاً، فإن كان مع الرداءة فيه قوة إحراقية، تكون النحاس، وإن كان غير شديد المخالطة بالزئبق، بل متداخلاً إياه سافاً فسافاً تولد الرصاص، وإن كان الزئبق والكبريت رديئين، فإن قوي التركيب، وفي الزئبق تخلخل أرضي وفي الكبريت إحراق تكون الحديد وإن ضعف التركيب تكون الأسرب، وأصحاب الصنعة يصححون هذه الدعاوي بعقد الزئبق بالكبريت انعقاداً محسوساً يحصل لهم بذلك غلبة الظن بأن الأحوال الطبيعية تقارب الأحوال الصناعية، وأما القطع فلا يد فيه لأحد.
And the indications testify that the matter of the seven bodies is mercury and sulfur, and the difference of types and varieties is due to the difference of their qualities and their mixtures, and the influence of one upon the other. As for the indications: it is that they, especially lead, melt into something like mercury, and mercury congeals by the smell of sulfur into something like lead, and mercury attaches to these bodies. Then mercury is composed of wateriness and sulfuriness. And the test of the craft science also bears witness to that. And the objection of Abu al-Barakat that if it were so, then each of mercury and sulfur would be found in the mine of the other, and in the mines of these bodies, is refuted by [the answer] that it is possible that the non-finding is due to their change through mixture, or due to lack of sensation because of the smallness of the parts. And as for the manner of their formation, it is that when mercury and sulfur are pure and the cooking of one by the other is complete, then if sulfur remains white and unburnt, silver forms; and if it is red and has a fine coloring power without burning, it forms gold; and if both are pure and in the sulfur there is a coloring power but it is reached before complete ripening by a freezing and congealing cold, it forms zinc; and if the mercury is pure and the sulfur is deficient, then if along with deficiency it has a burning power, it forms copper; and if it is not strongly mixed with mercury but rather interpenetrates it layer by layer, lead is generated; and if both mercury and sulfur are deficient, then if the composition is strong and in the mercury there is earthly looseness and in the sulfur there is burning, iron forms; and if the composition is weak, tin forms. And the craftsmen verify these claims by the congealing of mercury with sulfur in a perceptible congealing, obtaining by that a preponderance of opinion that natural states approximate industrial states. But certainty is not within anyone's power.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that the seven metals are composed of mercury (الزئبق) and sulfur (الكبريت), followed by an objection and its refutation, and a detailed account of the formation of each metal. The author begins by stating that indications (الأمارات) testify that the matter of the seven metals is mercury and sulfur, and that differences arise from their qualities and mixtures. The first indication is that lead melts into a mercury-like substance, and mercury congeals with sulfur's smell into lead-like substance. Also, mercury adheres to these bodies. Furthermore, mercury itself is composed of a watery and a sulfurous component. The craft of alchemy also testifies to this. Then the author mentions an objection by Abu al-Barakat: if metals were from mercury and sulfur, then one should find mercury and sulfur in the mines of the other and in the mines of these metals. The answer is that their absence may be due to their transformation through mixing, or because their tiny sizes evade sensation. Then the author describes the formation of each metal based on the purity and conditions of mercury and sulfur. He explains how silver, gold, zinc, copper, lead, iron, and tin are formed from variations of purity, burning power, mixture, and cooking. Craftsmen confirm these ideas by concretely congealing mercury with sulfur, obtaining a strong supposition (غلبة الظن) that natural processes resemble artificial ones. However, certainty (القطع) is not attainable by anyone.

غلبة الظن
القطع

Empirical verification yields strong probability but not absolute certainty in natural philosophy.

The Artificial Transmutation of Gold and Silver
Debate on possibility of making gold and silver by artifice, with Ibn Sina's objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments for and against artificial transmutation of gold and silver, and how the refutation uses analogies from natural generation and medicine.

Core Idea
The debate over alchemy hinges on whether essential forms can be produced without knowing them.

This passage presents a theological debate on whether humans can artificially produce gold and silver. The majority claim it is possible, while Ibn Sina skeptically demands knowledge of the essential differentiae. The response cleverly argues that even if we don't know the essence, we can still produce effects, as seen in nature and medicine.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical structure: claim, objection, response.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Find the position of 'most' (al-aktharūn) who affirm transmutation, and Ibn Sina's position of doubt.
Ibn Sina says possibility is not apparent, let alone occurrence.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's objection
Ibn Sina argues that essential differentiae are unknown, so they cannot be intentionally produced. He concedes superficial changes like dyeing but says these may not affect essence.
This is his main argument against even possibility.
4
Analyze the refutation's analogies
The refutation offers two points: bodies may not have essential differences, only accidents; and even if essences are unknown in detail, creation does not require detailed knowledge, as seen in natural generation (worm from hair, scorpion from basil-seed) and theriac.
The analogies show that effects can occur without knowing the cause in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وتكونها بالصنعة ) سيما الذهب والفضة مما يثبته الأكثرون، ويزعمون أن تحصيل صورها النوعية على تقدير ثبوتها غير مشروط بالعلم بحقائقها، وتفاصيلها، بل يكفي العلم المواد على وجه يستحق فيضان الصور بأسباب لا نعلمها]. بجميع يعني أن الكثير من العقلاء ذهبوا إلى أن تكون الذهب والفضة بالصنعة واقع . وذهب ابن سينا إلى أنه لم يظهر له إمكانه فضلاً عن الوقوع ، لأن الفصول الذاتية التي بها تصير هذه الأجساد أنواعاً أمور مجهولة والمجهول لا يمكن إيجاده. نعم. يمكن أن يصبغ النحاس بصبغ الفضة ، والفضة بصبغ الذهب، وأنه يزال عن الرصاص أكثر ما فيه من النقص، لكن هذه الأمور المحسوسة يجوز أن لا تكون هي الفصول. بل عوارض ولوازم. وأجيب بأنا لا ثم اختلاف الأجسام بالفصول والصور النوعية. بل متماثلة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض التي يمكن زوالها بالتدبير. هي ولو سلّم . فإن أريد لمجهولية الصور النوعية والفصول الذاتية، أنها مجهولة من كل وجه فممنوع . كيف وقد علم أنها مبادىء لهذه الخواص والأعراض. وإن أريد أنها مجهولة بحقائقها وتفاصيلها ، فلا نسلم أن الايجاد موقوف على العلم بذلك. وأنه لا يكفي العلم بجميع المواد على وجه حصل الظن بفيضان الصور عنده لأسباب لا تعلم على التفصيل كالحبة من الشعر، والعقرب من البازروج، ونحو ذلك، وكفى بصنعة الترياق ، وما فيه من الخواص والآثار شاهدا على إمكان ذلك نعم الكلام في الوقوع ، وفي العلم بجميع المواد، وتحصيل الاستعداد ولهذا جعل الكيمياء كالعنقاء مثلاً في اسم بلا مسمى.
He said: And their formation by artifice, especially gold and silver, which most affirm, claiming that the acquisition of their specific forms, on the assumption of their establishment, is not conditional on knowledge of their realities and details, but rather knowledge of all materials suffices in a way that deserves the emanation of forms by causes we do not know. That is, many intellectuals held that the formation of gold and silver by artifice is actual. Ibn Sina held that its possibility is not apparent to him, let alone its occurrence, because the specific differentiae by which these bodies become species are unknown matters, and the unknown cannot be brought into existence. Yes, one can dye copper with the dye of silver, and silver with the dye of gold, and remove from lead most of its deficiency, but these sensible matters may not be the differentiae, rather accidents and concomitants. And it was answered: We do not then affirm that bodies differ by differentiae and specific forms; rather they are similar, differing only by accidents that can be removed by management. And even if it is granted: if by the unknownness of specific forms and essential differentiae is meant that they are unknown from every aspect, it is denied. How? It is known that they are principles of these properties and accidents. If it is meant that they are unknown in their realities and details, we do not accept that bringing into existence is conditional on knowledge of that, and that knowledge of all materials does not suffice in a way that produces the conjecture of the emanation of forms due to causes not known in detail, like the worm from hair, and the scorpion from basil-seed, and the like. And the manufacture of theriac (tiryāq) and what it contains of properties and effects suffices as a witness to the possibility of that. Yes, the discussion is about occurrence, and about knowledge of all materials and the acquisition of preparation; therefore alchemy is made like the phoenix, an example of a name without a named thing.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the possibility of artificially producing gold and silver. It first states the position of "most" (al-aktharūn) who affirm that such transmutation is real, and that knowledge of the specific forms is not required, only preparation of matter. Then it presents Ibn Sina's objection: he doubts even possibility, because the essential differentiae that make bodies species are unknown, and one cannot create what one does not know. He concedes that superficial changes like dyeing are possible but may not affect the essence. The refutation counters with two points: (1) bodies may not have essential differences at all, only accidental distinctions that can be altered by human intervention; (2) even if essences are unknown in detail, creation does not require detailed knowledge — as seen in natural generation (e.g., maggots from hair, scorpions from basil-seed) and in the artificial production of theriac, which has specific effects without full knowledge of its essence. The passage concludes that the real difficulty is not possibility but actual occurrence and the practical knowledge of materials and preparation, likening alchemy to the mythical phoenix — a name without a referent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina categorically denies the possibility of artificial transmutation.
Correction: The text shows Ibn Sina only says possibility is not apparent to him ('لم يظهر له إمكانه'), and he doubts both possibility and occurrence, but the refutation treats it as a debate about possibility, not an outright denial.

Checkpoint

C) He thinks alchemy is forbidden by religion.
D) He argues that gold and silver are too pure to be made.

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that the possibility of artificial transmutation is defended by appealing to natural processes where effects occur without full knowledge of essences.

Ibn Sina doubts that we can produce what we do not fully understand. In what areas of your life—your worship, your work, your relationships—are you trying to achieve results without knowing the deeper realities? What would it take to move from mere action to understanding?
?
The passage ends by comparing alchemy to the phoenix: a name without a thing. Are there aspects of your faith or practice that have become mere labels—words you use without the corresponding reality? What is one concrete step you can take today to ensure your Islam is not just a name but a lived truth?
?

Disappearance of Composite Forms in Gold Formation
Al-Taftazani explains why mercury and sulfur forms vanish in gold formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that the forms of mercury and sulfur completely disappear when gold is formed, and how this explains gold's distinct volume and weight.

Core Idea
Gold does not preserve the forms of its ingredients; their substances fully transform.

This passage from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a key question in medieval chemistry: when gold is formed from mercury and sulfur, do the original materials retain their forms? The author explains the agreed position that they do not, and provides a rationale based on the nature of mixture and the resulting properties. We'll examine the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the key terms like 'زوال صور' and 'مزاج'.
The passage uses technical language from the natural philosophy tradition.
2
Understand the argument's core
Identify why al-Taftazani says the forms disappear. The reason is that they are 'تابعة للمزاج المنعدم عند تصغر الأجزاء جداً' — the mixture ceases when parts become very small. This is the mechanism.
This connects to the consequence about volume and weight.
3
Contrast with other mixtures
Notice that al-Taftazani contrasts this with mixtures that remain on the forms of their parts (like a composite of bodies). This distinction supports the argument that gold's properties are not intermediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واتفقوا على زوال صور المواد المركبة كالزئبق والكبريت عند تكون الذهب، لكونها تابعة للمزاج المنعدم عند تصغر الأجزاء جداً، ولهذا لا يكون حجم الذهب ووزنه بين حجم الزئبق والكبريت ووزنيهما، كما هو حكم المركبات الباقية على صور أجزائها. يريد أن المزاج الثاني ليس كالأول في بقاء الأجزاء؛ أعني البسائط العنصرية على صورها النوعية، بل المواد المركبة كالزئبق والكبريت المتكون منهما الذهب لا تبقى على صورها لكونها تابعة للمزاج المنعدم عند تصغر الأجزاء جداً. فالتركيب المفضي إلى حصول المزاج التابع لتصغر الأجزاء، لا كتركيب الشخص من الأعضاء، لا يكون عند التحقيق إلا من البسائط العنصرية، ولهذا لا يكون حجم الذهب ووزنه بين حجم الزئبق والكبريت ووزنيهما، على ما هو قياس المركب من الأجسام المختلفة في الثقل الباقية على صورها، بل حجمه أقل منهما بكثير ووزنه أكثر على ما سيأتي.
He said: And they agreed on the disappearance of the forms of composite materials like mercury and sulfur when gold is formed, because they are subject to the mixture that is annihilated when the parts become very small, and therefore the volume and weight of gold are not between the volume and weight of mercury and sulfur, as is the rule for composites that remain on the forms of their parts. He intends that the second mixture is not like the first in the persistence of the parts; I mean the elemental simples on their specific forms, rather composite materials like mercury and sulfur from which gold is formed do not remain on their forms because they are subject to the annihilated mixture when the parts become very small. So the composition that leads to obtaining the mixture subject to the smallness of parts, not like the composition of a person from organs, is only, upon investigation, from elemental simples. Therefore the volume and weight of gold are not between the volume and weight of mercury and sulfur, as is the measure of a composite from bodies different in weight that remain on their forms, but its volume is much less than both, and its weight more, as will come.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage discusses the transformation of mercury and sulfur into gold. It states that scholars have agreed that the forms of composite materials (like mercury and sulfur) disappear when gold is formed because they are subject to a mixture that ceases to exist when the parts become very small. This explains why gold's volume and weight are not intermediate between those of mercury and sulfur, which would be the case if the composites persisted. The author clarifies that the mixture in gold formation is not like a mixture that preserves the parts; rather, the composite materials lose their specific forms. Thus, the composition that leads to gold is only from elemental simples (not retaining the intermediate forms). Therefore, gold's volume is much smaller and its weight much larger than either mercury or sulfur, contrary to what would be expected if they remained as mixtures.

Key Terms
زوال صور
مواد مركبة
زئبق
كبريت
مزاج
بسائط عنصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gold formation preserves the forms of its constituent materials (mercury and sulfur).
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the forms of mercury and sulfur completely disappear when gold is formed, due to the annihilation of the mixture when parts become very small.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Conclusion on Weight, Buoyancy, and Water Displacement
Al-Taftazani concludes with principles of specific gravity, buoyancy, and an example with gold and silver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relationship between an object's weight, volume, and buoyancy as explained by Al-Taftazani, including the principles of specific gravity and Archimedes' principle.

Core Idea
Buoyancy is determined by the weight of displaced water, not just the object's own weight.

This concluding passage ties together the principles of body weight, volume, and floating. It provides a clear example with gold and silver to illustrate how the weight of displaced water determines whether an object sinks or floats. This is a direct application of what we now call Archimedes' principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms and the flow from general principle to specific example.
Pay attention to the numbers given for gold and silver.
2
Understand the general principle
The passage begins by stating that bodies differ in weight due to differences in their forms (صور). This leads to differences in volume and in floating/sinking behavior.
3
Follow the example with gold and silver
The example gives specific values: 100 mithqals of gold displace 5.25 mithqals of water, while silver displaces 9 2/3 mithqals. Understand the ratio relationships.
The ratio of displaced water equals the ratio of volumes.
4
Apply the rule for sinking and floating
From the rule that an object's weight in water equals its weight in air minus the weight of displaced water, we derive the conditions: if displaced water weight is less, more, or equal to the object's weight.
Neutral buoyancy occurs when they are equal.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
This passage is a concise summary of ancient understanding of buoyancy. Reflect on how it aligns with modern physics and why it appears in a theological work.
It demonstrates the application of causal reasoning in natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: خاتمة. ويتعين جميع ذلك بتعين الأجسام تتفاوت في الثقل لاختلاف الصور، وبحسب ذلك تتفاوت في الحجم والحيز، وفي الطفو على الماء والرسوب فيه، ويختلف وزن كل في الماء والهواء. مثلاً ماء مائة مثقال من الذهب خمسة مثاقيل وربع، ومن فضة تسعة وثلثان، ونسبة ماء الذهب إلى ماء الفضة نسبة حجمه إلى حجمها، وثقلها إلى ثقله. وإذا سقط ماء كل عن وزنه في الهواء بقي وزنه في الماء، وما كان ماؤه أقل من وزنه فهو راسب، أو أكثر فطاف، وإن تساويا نزل فيه بحيث يماس أعلاه سطح الماء.
He said: Conclusion. And all that is determined by the determination of bodies that differ in weight due to the difference of forms, and accordingly they differ in volume and space, and in floating on water and sinking in it. The weight of each varies in water and air. For example, the water of one hundred mithqals of gold is five and a quarter mithqals, and of silver is nine and two thirds mithqals. The ratio of the water of gold to the water of silver is the ratio of its volume to its volume, and the ratio of its weight to its weight. And when the water of each is subtracted from its weight in air, its weight remains in water. And whatever has its water less than its weight, it is sinking; or more, it floats; and if they are equal, it descends into it such that its top touches the surface of the water.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion on the principles of specific gravity and buoyancy. It states that bodies differ in weight due to differences in their forms, and accordingly they differ in volume and in whether they float or sink in water. The weight of an object varies in water and air. An example is given: 100 mithqals of gold displace 5.25 mithqals of water, while silver displaces 9 2/3 mithqals. The ratio of the displaced water volumes equals the ratio of the volumes of the objects. Then it states that when the weight of displaced water is subtracted from the object's weight in air, the result is its weight in water (i.e., apparent weight). If the weight of displaced water is less than the object's weight, the object sinks; if more, it floats; if equal, it suspends with its top touching the water surface. This is a clear application of Archimedes' principle.

Key Terms
ثقل
حجم
طفو
رسوب
ماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Buoyancy depends only on the weight of the object.
Correction: Buoyancy depends on the relationship between the object's weight and the weight of the water it displaces, as shown by the criteria for sinking, floating, and neutral buoyancy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the weight of the displaced water equals the object's weight in air, what happens?
A) The object floats completely
B) The object sinks to the bottom
C) The object descends until its top touches the water surface
D) The object remains suspended underwater

Key Takeaway
The buoyancy of an object depends on whether its weight exceeds the weight of the water it displaces, a principle that Al-Taftazani summarizes with the example of gold and silver.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes precise ratios in the displacement of water by gold and silver. When you see such exactness in the natural world, do you pause to consider the Creator behind it? How does that reflection affect your reliance on material things?
?
In your own life, what 'weights' (sins, distractions) are causing you to sink away from Allah? And what 'water displacement' (sincere worship, repentance) can you increase to lighten that burden?
?
Al-Taftazani derived a clear rule from observation: weight in water equals weight in air minus displaced water. Do you apply similar critical thinking to your spiritual state, subtracting your shortcomings to see your true standing before Allah? What concrete step will you take today to measure your spiritual buoyancy?

The True Cause of Weight Variation: Forms, Not Void
Al-Taftazani states the cause of weight variation and rejects void-based explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Taftazani, the variation in weight among bodies is due to differences in their forms and dispositions, not to the quantity of parts or the presence of void.

Core Idea
Forms, not void, determine weight differences.

Al-Taftazani begins this passage by clarifying the fundamental cause of weight variation. He argues that the common view attributing weight differences to the amount of matter or void space is incorrect. Instead, the key factor is the form (sura) and disposition of each body. This principle sets the stage for understanding all subsequent physical properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Taftazani states that bodies vary in heaviness due to differences in forms.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لاختلاف الصور'.
2
Identify the rejected view
The author explicitly rejects the idea that weight differences come from the abundance or scarcity of parts with void. Locate this negation in the text.
3
Connect to previous discussion
The text refers to a previous indication ('سبقت إشارة'). Understand that this is not a new idea but a reinforcement of an earlier point.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for physics and theology. It links weight to intrinsic forms, not mere materialism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعين جميع ذلك بتعين الأجسام تتفاوت في الثقل لاختلاف الصور، وبحسب ذلك تتفاوت في الحجم والحيز، وفي الطفو على الماء والرسوب فيه، ويختلف وزن كل في الماء والهواء. وقد سبقت إشارة إلى أن اختلاف الأجسام في الخفة والثقل عائد إلى اختلافها في الصور والاستعدادات، لا إلى كثرة الأجزاء وقلتها مع تخلل الخلاء. وبحسب تفاوتها في الخفة والثقل، تتفاوت فيما يتبع ذلك من الحجم والحيز والطفو على الماء والرسوب فيه، ومن اختلاف أوزانها في الماء، بعد التساوي في الهواء. مثلا حجم الأخف يكون أعظم من حجم الأقل مع التساوي في الوزن، كمائة مثقال من الفضة، ومائة من الذهب، وحيز الأخف يكون إلى صوب المحيط، والأثقل إلى صوب المركز، وإن تساويا وزناً أو حجماً، والأخف قد يعلو الماء، والأثقل يرسب فيه كالخشب والحديد وإن كان وزن الخشب أضعاف وزن الحديد. وإذا كان في إحدى كفتي الميزان مائة مثقال من الحجر، وفي الأخرى مائة مثقال من الذهب أو الفضة أو غيرهما من الأجساد التي جوهرها أثقل من جوهر الحجر ولا محالة يقوم الميزان مستوياً في الهواء، فإذا أرسلنا الكفتين في الماء لم يبق الاستواء، بل يميل العمود إلى جانب الجوهر الأثقل، وكلما كان من جوهر أثقل، كان الميل أكثر ويفتقر الاستواء إلى زيادة في الحجر حسب زيادة الثقل، مع أن وزن الجوهر ليس إلا مائة مثقال مثلاً، وذلك لأن الأثقل أقدر على خرق القوام الأغلظ، وأما إذا أرسلنا أحدهما فقط في الماء، فالعمود يميل إلى جانب الهواء، لكونه أرق قواماً. وقد حاول أبو ريحان تعيين مقدار تفاوت ما بين الفلزات، وبعض الأحجار في الحجم، وفي الخفة والثقل، بأن عمل إناء على شكل الطبرزد مركباً على عنقه شبه ميزاب منحني، كما يكون حال الأباريق، وملأه ماء وأرسل فيه مائة مثقال من الفضة.
All that is determined by the determination of bodies: bodies vary in heaviness due to differences in forms, and accordingly they vary in volume and space, and in floating on water and sinking in it, and the weight of each differs in water and air. And a reference has preceded that the difference of bodies in lightness and heaviness is due to their difference in forms and dispositions, not to the abundance or scarcity of parts with the interpolation of void. And according to their variation in lightness and heaviness, they vary in what follows that of volume, space, floating on water, and sinking in it, and of the difference of their weights in water, after equality in air. For example, the volume of the lighter is greater than the volume of the less heavy when they are equal in weight, like one hundred mithqals of silver and one hundred of gold; and the space of the lighter is toward the direction of the circumference, and the heavier toward the direction of the center, even if they are equal in weight or volume; and the lighter may rise above water, and the heavier sinks in it, like wood and iron even if the weight of the wood is many times the weight of iron. And if there is in one of the two pans of the balance one hundred mithqals of stone, and in the other one hundred mithqals of gold or silver or other bodies whose substance is heavier than the substance of stone, without doubt the balance is level in air; but when we immerse the two pans in water, the level does not remain, rather the beam inclines toward the side of the heavier substance, and the more a substance is heavier, the greater the inclination, and levelness requires an increase in the stone according to the increase in heaviness, even though the weight of the substance is only one hundred mithqals for example. That is because the heavier is more capable of penetrating the denser medium. And if we immerse only one of them in water, the beam inclines toward the side of the air, because it is of finer texture. And Abu Rayhan attempted to determine the amount of difference between metals and some stones in volume and in lightness and heaviness, by making a vessel in the shape of a flagon, with a sort of curved spout attached to its neck, like the case of ewers, and filled it with water and placed in it one hundred mithqals of silver.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion on the physical properties of bodies, specifically weight, volume, and buoyancy. The author, al-Taftazani, begins by reinstating that the differences in heaviness (thiqal) among bodies are due to differences in their forms (suwar), not to the quantity of matter or the presence of void. He then explains how this affects volume, space, floating, and sinking. He provides an example: when equal weights of silver and gold are compared, the lighter (silver) has a greater volume. The lighter tends toward the circumference, the heavier toward the center. He then discusses buoyancy: lighter objects float, heavier sink, even if the lighter object has a greater weight (e.g., wood versus iron). Next, he describes a balance experiment: two pans with equal weights of stone and gold are balanced in air, but when immersed in water, the pan with the heavier substance (gold) sinks more because it is better at penetrating the denser medium (water). He notes that if only one pan is immersed, the beam tilts toward the air side. Finally, he mentions that Abu Rayhan al-Biruni attempted to precisely measure these differences using a special water-displacement vessel.

Key Terms
ثقل
خفة
صور
استعدادات
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Weight differences are caused by the amount of matter (more parts = heavier) or the presence of void (less dense = lighter).
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly denies this, stating that weight variation is due to forms and dispositions, not quantity of parts or void.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary cause of variation in heaviness among bodies?
A) Differences in the number of parts
B) Differences in forms and dispositions
C) The presence of void
D) Differences in size alone

Key Takeaway
Weight differences are rooted in the forms of bodies, not in material quantity or void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you been measuring your 'weight' by quantity—how much you do, how much you own—rather than the 'form' of your intentions and dispositions? What would it mean to let the form of taqwā (God-consciousness) determine the true heaviness of your deeds?
?
Consider a difficulty you are carrying. This lesson teaches that the cause of weight is not the amount of 'parts' but the inner form. What 'form' of your heart—pride, despair, or reliance on God—is actually making your burden heavy? Are you willing to ask God to change that form?
?
You have just read that bodies differ in weight due to their forms, not due to void. In your spiritual life, have you ever attributed your lightness to a lack of 'void' (empty moments) rather than a lack of proper form? Today, what one 'form' of virtue do you need to actively adopt to become heavier with sincerity?

Volume, Buoyancy, and the Behaviour of Lighter and Heavier Bodies
Al-Taftazani explains the consequences of weight variation and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how differences in lightness and heaviness lead to differences in volume, spatial tendency, and buoyancy, as illustrated by the examples of silver/gold and wood/iron.

Core Idea
Lighter bodies have greater volume and tend upward; heavier bodies sink.

Having established the principle that forms cause weight differences, al-Taftazani now explains the observable consequences. He shows that lighter objects have a larger volume for the same weight, and they tend toward the periphery, while heavier objects tend toward the center. He then discusses buoyancy, using the striking example of wood and iron, where the much heavier wood can still float.

Guided Path
1
Read the general statement
Read the first sentence that states that according to variation in lightness and heaviness, bodies vary in volume, space, floating, sinking, and weight in water.
Notice the phrase 'تتفاوت فيما يتبع ذلك'.
2
Analyze the silver-gold example
Compare equal weights of silver and gold. The lighter silver has a greater volume. Also note the spatial tendency: lighter toward circumference, heavier toward center.
3
Analyze the wood-iron example
Wood is lighter than iron, so wood floats even if its weight is many times that of iron. This shows buoyancy is not simply about weight.
This challenges the naive view that heavier objects always sink.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these examples support the idea that form, not mere quantity, determines behavior.

What does the example of wood and iron demonstrate?

Lighter bodies have larger volumes and tend upward; buoyancy depends on the nature of the substance, not just total weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The wooden block sinks in water despite its total weight? No—it floats because of its essential nature. Where in your life are you weighed down by external burdens (wealth, status) when your essential nature is meant to rise?
?
Consider the two types of weight: total weight (accidental) and essential lightness. In your pursuit of good deeds, do you focus on the volume and number (like heavy weight) or the intrinsic sincerity (like essential lightness)? Which actually lifts you higher with Allah?

The Balance Experiment and Biruni's Measurement of Density
Al-Taftazani describes a balance experiment and Biruni's density measurement method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the balance experiment that reveals weight differences in water, and how Biruni attempted to measure the volume and weight differences of metals.

Core Idea
Immersing objects in water reveals hidden weight differences due to substance density.

In this final part, al-Taftazani describes a clever balance experiment that demonstrates how objects that balance in air become imbalanced when immersed in water. This proves that heavier substances (like gold) are more capable of penetrating the denser medium. He then mentions Abu Rayhan al-Biruni's attempt to precisely measure these differences using a water-displacement vessel.

Guided Path
1
Read the balance experiment
Understand the setup: two pans with equal weights of stone and gold balance in air, but when immersed in water, the pan with gold dips lower.
Note the cause: gold is 'more capable of penetrating the denser medium'.
2
Consider the one-pan immersion
If only one pan is immersed, the beam tilts toward the air side because air is finer in texture.
3
Read about Biruni's experiment
Biruni used a special vessel with a spout to measure the volume of water displaced by a known weight of silver.
This is an early method for determining density.
4
Reflect on the significance
These experiments show how empirical observation can confirm theoretical principles about forms and substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When life 'immerses' you in trials, what is the 'density' of your faith? Does it sink you into despair or cause you to rise above? Compare your reaction to the behavior of gold versus stone in water.
?
Biruni measured physical density with precision. How precisely do you measure the 'density' of your daily deeds—are they light and airy or substantial and heavy in the sight of Allah?
?
The balance experiment shows that outward equality (same weight) hides inner difference (density). Where in your life might you be fooled by outward appearances—whether of yourself or others—and need to look deeper at the inner substance?

Causes of Lightness and Heaviness
Explains the cause of lightness/heaviness and its observable effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that differences in lightness and heaviness among bodies are due to their forms and preparations, not to the abundance or scarcity of parts or the void, and how this principle explains various phenomena like volume, space, and behavior in water.

Core Idea
Lightness and heaviness are due to form and preparation, not size or void.

Al-Taftazani sets out a foundational principle of natural philosophy: why are some bodies lighter and others heavier? He rejects the idea that it is merely a matter of more or fewer parts with void in between. Instead, he attributes it to differences in forms (suwar) and dispositions (isti'dadat). This principle then explains a range of observable phenomena, from volume differences to floating and sinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on causality
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the statement of the cause of lightness and heaviness. Then read the translation.
Notice that the author explicitly rejects the void-based explanation.
2
Identify the consequences
The passage lists several consequences of the principle: differences in volume, space, floating/sinking, and behavior in water balance. Find each one.
The examples illustrate the principle.
3
Reflect on the rejection of the void
Consider why the author dismisses the idea that lightness/heaviness is due to the number of parts or void. What philosophical stance does this imply?
This connects to broader debates in Islamic natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعرفة مقدار كل منهما في المركب مع بقاء التركيب، وفي عمل الموازين القريبة جعله خاتمة بحث المعدنيات، لأن أمره فيها أظهر، واحتياجها إليه أكثر وقد سبقت إشارة إلى أن اختلاف الأجسام في الخفة والثقل عائد إلى اختلافها في الصور والاستعدادات، لا إلى كثرة الأجزاء وقلتها مع تخلل الخلاء، وبحسب تفاوتها في الخفة والثقل، تتفاوت فيما يتبع ذلك من الحجم والحيز والطفو على الماء والرسوب فيه، ومن اختلاف أوزانها في الماء، بعد التساوي في الهواء . مثلا حجم الأخف يكون أعظم من حجم الأقل مع التساوي في الوزن، كمائة مثقال من الفضة، ومائة من الذهب، وحيز الأخف يكون إلى صوب المحيط، والأثقل إلى صوب المركز، وإن تساويا وزناً أو حجماً، والأخف قد يعلو الماء، والأثقل يرسب فيه كالخشب والحديد وإن كان وزن الخشب أضعاف وزن الحديد، وإذا كان في إحدى كفتي الميزان مائة مثقال من الحجر، وفي الأخرى مائة مثقال من الذهب أو الفضة أو غيرهما من الأجساد التي جوهرها أثقل من جوهر الحجر ولا محالة يقوم الميزان مستوياً في الهواء، فإذا أرسلنا الكفتين في الماء لم يبق الاستواء. بل يميل العمود إلى جانب الجوهر الأثقل ، وكلما كان من جوهر أثقل، كان الميل أكثر ويفتقر الاستواء إلى زيادة في الحجر حسب زيادة الثقل، مع أن وزن الجوهر ليس إلا مائة مثقال مثلاً ، وذلك لأن الأثقل أقدر على خرق القوام الأغلظ، وأما إذا أرسلنا أحدهما فقط في الماء، فالعمود يميل إلى جانب الهواء، لكونه أرق قواماً. وقد حاول أبو ريحان تعيين مقدار تفاوت ما بين الفلزات، وبعض الأحجار في الحجم، وفي الخفة والثقل، بأن عمل إناء على شكل الطبرزد مركباً على عنقه شبه میزاب منحني، كما يكون حال الأباريق، وملأه ماء وأرسل فيه مائة مثقال من الذهب مثلاً، وجعل تحت رأس الميزاب كفة الميزان الذي يريد به معرفة مقدار الماء الذي يخرج من الإناء، وهكذا كل من الفلزات والأحجار، بعد ما بالغ في تنقية الفلزات من الغش، وفي تصفية الماء، وكان ذلك من ماء جيحون في خوارزم في فصل الخريف. ولا شك أن الحكم يختلف باختلاف المياه ، و اختلاف أحوالها بحسب البلدان والفصول فحصل معرفة مقدار الماء الذي يخرج من الإناء بمائة مثقال من كل من الفلزات والأحجار، وعرف بذلك مقدار
And knowing the measure of each of them in the compound while composition remains, and in the working of near balances, he made it the conclusion of the discussion of minerals, because its matter is more apparent in them and their need for it is greater. And an indication has preceded that the difference of bodies in lightness and heaviness is due to their difference in forms and preparations, not to the abundance or scarcity of parts with the interposition of the void. And according to their disparity in lightness and heaviness, they differ in what follows from that of volume, space, floating on water and sinking in it, and of the difference of their weights in water after equality in air. For example, the volume of the lighter is greater than the volume of the less (dense) when the weight is equal, like one hundred mithqals of silver and one hundred of gold. And the space of the lighter is towards the side of the circumference, and the heavier towards the center, even if they are equal in weight or volume. And the lighter may rise over water, and the heavier sinks in it, like wood and iron, even if the weight of wood is many times the weight of iron. And if in one pan of the balance there is one hundred mithqals of stone, and in the other one hundred mithqals of gold or silver or other bodies whose substance is heavier than the substance of stone, necessarily the balance stands level in air. But if we immerse both pans in water, the equilibrium does not remain; rather, the beam inclines towards the side of the heavier substance, and whenever the substance is heavier, the inclination is greater, and equilibrium requires an increase in stone according to the increase in weight, even though the weight of the substance is only one hundred mithqals, for instance. And that is because the heavier is more capable of piercing the denser medium. And as for if we immerse only one of them in water, the beam inclines towards the side of air, because it is thinner in medium. And Abu Rayhan attempted to determine the measure of disparity among metals and some stones in volume and in lightness and heaviness, by making a vessel in the shape of a long-necked bottle, fitted on its neck with a sort of curved spout, as is the case with ewers, and filled it with water, and sent into it one hundred mithqals of gold, for example, and placed under the head of the spout the pan of the balance by which he wanted to know the amount of water that comes out of the vessel. And likewise for each of the metals and stones, after he was thorough in purifying the metals from impurity and in filtering the water, and that was from the water of Jayhun in Khwarazm in autumn. And there is no doubt that the judgment varies with the variation of waters and their states according to regions and seasons. So he obtained knowledge of the amount of water that comes out of the vessel with one hundred mithqals of each of the metals and stones, and he knew thereby the measure
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory and historical discussion by al-Taftazani on the causes of lightness and heaviness in bodies, and includes a description of an experiment by Abu Rayhan al-Biruni to measure specific gravity. The passage begins by stating that the study of measuring lightness and heaviness in compounds and in balances is placed as the conclusion of the discussion of minerals because it is most evident and needed in that field. The author then recalls an earlier point: that differences in lightness and heaviness are due to differences in forms (صور) and preparations (استعدادات), not to the number of parts or the presence of void (خلاء). This is a key principle of Aristotelian physics. Then he explains several consequences of this principle. First, volume: for equal weights, the lighter body has a larger volume (e.g., silver is lighter than gold, so 100 mithqals of silver occupies more volume than 100 mithqals of gold). Second, space (حيز): the lighter tends towards the circumference (outer region), the heavier towards the center (earth's center). Third, floating and sinking: the lighter may float on water while the heavier sinks, even if the lighter has much greater weight (e.g., wood floats, iron sinks, even if wooden block is heavier than an iron nail). Fourth, the behavior in a balance when immersed in water: if both pans contain equal weights of different substances (e.g., stone vs. gold), they balance in air, but when immersed in water, the pan with the heavier substance (gold) goes down because it is denser and thus has less buoyancy. The author explains that the heavier substance is more capable of piercing the denser medium (water). If only one pan is immersed, the beam tilts towards the air side because air is a thinner medium. Then the passage introduces al-Biruni's experiment: he made a vessel shaped like a bottle with a curved spout, filled it with water, and placed a known weight (100 mithqals) of a metal into the water. The displaced water was collected in a pan of a balance. He repeated this for various metals and stones, carefully purifying them and using filtered water from the Jayhun river in Khwarazm in autumn (to control temperature and purity). He then determined the volume of water displaced by each substance, thus knowing their specific gravities. The author notes that the result varies with the type of water and its conditions, so al-Biruni's measurements are relative.

Key Terms
خفة
ثقل
الصور
الاستعدادات
الخلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Weight differences are caused by the amount of matter (parts) with void making something lighter.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that lightness/heaviness is due to forms and preparations, not to the quantity of parts or void.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the cause of differences in lightness and heaviness among bodies?
A) The abundance or scarcity of parts with void
B) Differences in forms and preparations
C) The amount of air trapped inside
D) The shape of the body

Key Takeaway
The lightness or heaviness of a body is determined by its form and preparation, not by its size or void content.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The text teaches that a body's lightness or heaviness comes from its inner form and preparation, not its size or accumulation of parts. Look at your own spiritual state: What 'form' (intention, belief, character) truly determines whether your heart rises towards Allah or sinks into neglect? Are you relying on the 'size' of your deeds while ignoring the inner sincerity? Name one area where you need to change your 'preparation' to become lighter in worship.
?
You learned that the heavier substance is better at piercing a denser medium. In your life, what 'heavy' attachments (pride, wealth, status) make you sink in worldly concerns? How can you transform your inner form to become lighter, so you rise towards what truly matters? What is one concrete step you can take today to adjust your 'preparation'?
?
The passage shows that equal weights can behave differently depending on their substance. Reflect: You and another person may perform the same outward acts, but your inner forms differ. Are you more focused on the outer weight of your actions or on cultivating the right inner form? What aspect of your character needs the most urgent reformation to bring sincerity and humility?

Al-Biruni's Experiment for Specific Gravity
Describes al-Biruni's experimental setup for measuring specific gravity by water displacement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the experimental method al-Biruni used to compare the densities of metals and stones by water displacement.

Core Idea
Al-Biruni measured specific gravity by collecting water displaced by equal weights of different substances.

After establishing the theoretical principles, al-Taftazani describes an actual experiment by the great scholar Abu Rayhan al-Biruni. Al-Biruni designed a vessel with a spout to collect displaced water, allowing him to compare the volumes of equal weights of various metals and stones. This is one of the earliest known methods for determining specific gravity.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the apparatus
Read the Arabic passage describing the vessel shape and spout. Visualize the setup: a long-necked bottle with a curved spout, similar to a ewer.
The word 'طبرزد' refers to a long-necked bottle.
2
Understand the procedure
Al-Biruni fills the vessel with water, then immerses a known weight (100 mithqals) of a metal. The displaced water flows out through the spout and is collected in a balance pan. He repeats this for each metal and stone.
This measures the volume indirectly by the weight of displaced water.
3
Note the controls
Al-Biruni purified the metals, filtered the water, and used water from the Jayhun river in a specific season. Why might these controls matter?
Temperature and purity affect water density, so controlling them increases accuracy.

Does Diversity of Action Imply Diversity of Powers?
Argument that diverse actions do not necessitate distinct faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why observable differences in actions do not necessarily prove the existence of multiple distinct faculties, and how this principle relates to the study of the four faculties.

Core Idea
Different actions can arise from a single power; diversity of instruments explains apparent multiplicity.

This lesson examines a key argument in the discussion of the four faculties. The author addresses a potential misunderstanding: just because we see different actions does not mean we must posit distinct powers. Instead, the differences may be due to the instruments and dispositions. Let's read the passage and unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the author's claim that different actions can proceed from a single power.
Notice the phrase 'ولا يخفي أنه لا يدل على تعدد القوى بالذات' which directly states the conclusion.
2
Understand the example of organs
The author gives the example of organs being weak in some actions and strong in others. Reflect on how this illustrates the principle.
Consider why this observation does not force the conclusion of multiple powers.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
The passage argues that variation in action does not prove multiplicity of powers in essence, because it could be due to instruments and dispositions. Ask yourself why this is an important principle in kalam.
This principle prevents inferring essential distinctions from accidental variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني: وهو بيان تغاير هذه القوى فمبني على ما تقرر عندهم من صدور الأفعال المختلفة عن قوة واحدة طبيعية. ولهذا ترى بعض الأعضاء ضعيفاً في بعض هذه الأفعال وقوياً في الباقي، ولا يخفي أنه لا يدل على تعدد القوى بالذات، لجواز أن يكون الاختلاف عائداً إلى اختلاف الآلات والاستعدادات.
As for the second: it is an explanation of the difference (tamāyuz) of these powers, and it is based on what has been established among them: that different actions proceed from a single natural power. For this reason you see some organs weak in some of these actions and strong in the rest, and it is not hidden that this does not indicate multiplicity of powers in essence, because it is possible that the difference is due to the difference of instruments and dispositions.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses the second point: showing the distinction (tamāyuz) of these four faculties. The author states that the principle is based on the established rule that different actions can proceed from a single natural power. For example, some organs are weak in certain actions but strong in others. This does not necessarily prove multiple distinct powers in essence, because the difference could be attributed to differences in instruments and dispositions (i.e., the physical structures and conditions). Thus, observable variation in action is not conclusive evidence for multiplicity of powers.

Key Terms
تغاير القوى
قوة واحدة
آلات
استعدادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That different actions always imply different powers.
Correction: The passage shows that actions can differ due to instruments and dispositions, not necessarily multiple powers in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why might an organ be weak in one action and strong in another?
A) Due to having multiple distinct powers
B) Due to difference in instruments and dispositions
C) Due to the presence of all four faculties equally
D) Due to external compulsion

Key Takeaway
Observable differences in actions do not prove distinct faculties; they may be due to instruments and dispositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice a personal weakness in one area (e.g., patience) but strength in another (e.g., generosity), do you assume that these are completely separate 'traits' you were given? Or can you see them as different expressions of a single power – your soul – limited by your habits (instruments) and current dispositions? Will you stop labeling yourself as 'naturally impatient' and instead ask: 'What instrument (practice, environment, intention) can I adjust so that the same power produces patience?'
?
This lesson teaches that observable diversity does not prove essential multiplicity. Think of a time you judged someone's character as 'divided' because they acted inconsistently – strong in one virtue, weak in another. Did you assume they had two different souls or faculties? If a single power can produce varied actions due to different instruments, does that change how you view your own inconsistencies? Will you begin to treat each weakness as a call to refine your instruments and dispositions, rather than a verdict on your essence?
?
If Allah created you with one essential soul (or power) that manifests through many instruments, then every strength and weakness points back to the same root. Which instrument in your life – your tongue, your heart, your habits – is currently producing actions you dislike? Are you willing to stop blaming 'lack of willpower' (as if you need another power) and instead examine the state of your instruments and dispositions? Commit to one specific instrument (e.g., your tongue) and one disposition (e.g., humility) to work on today.

The Four Faculties in Every Organ and Their Multiplication
Explanation that all four faculties exist in every organ, with stomach as example of multiplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the four faculties (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) are present in every organ and may be multiplied in organs related to nutrition, as illustrated by the stomach's dual sets.

Core Idea
Every organ needs all four faculties, but some organs have multiple sets.

In this passage, the author builds on the earlier discussion of the four faculties by asserting that they are not confined to a few organs but are present in every organ. Moreover, in organs that serve as 'instruments of food,' like the stomach, these faculties are multiplied to handle both processing food for the body and nourishing the organ itself. The stomach example vividly shows two parallel sets of faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the claim that the four faculties exist in every organ and can be multiplied.
Pay attention to the phrase 'tujadu al-arba'u fi kulli 'udw'.
2
Identify the two sets in the stomach
The passage describes two sets of the four faculties in the stomach: one set for processing food for the body, and another set for processing blood for the stomach's own nutrition. List each set from the text.
The first set deals with food from the mouth; the second set deals with blood from the liver.
3
Understand why multiplication occurs
The stomach is an 'instrument of food' (ālat al-ghidhā'), so it needs additional faculties to handle its own nutrition while also serving the body. This is the principle behind multiplication.
This principle likely applies to other organs like the liver and veins mentioned at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وتوجد الأربع في كل عضو وقد يتضاعف في البعض. يعني الجاذبة والماسكة والهاضمة والدافعة في كل عضو لأنه يفتقر في البقاء إلى الاغتذاء المفتقر إلى الأفعال المستندة إلى القوى الأربع، وقد يتضاعف في بعض الأعضاء أعني التي هي آلات الغذاء كالمعدة، فإن فيها جاذبة للغذاء من الفم، وماسكة له فيها، ومغيرة إلى ما يصلح أن يصير دماً في الكبد، ودافعة للفضلات إلى الأمعاء، ثم جاذبة للدم الذي يصير غذاء لجوهر المعدة كسائر الأعضاء، وماسكة له ريثما يغير إلى مشاكلة جوهر المعدة، وهاضمة تفعل ذلك، ودافعة لما يخالط ذلك من غير الملائم. وكذا الكبد والعروق.
He said: And the four exist in every organ, and may be multiplied in some. Meaning the attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive [are] in every organ because it depends, in remaining [alive], on nutrition which depends on the actions based on the four powers. And they may be multiplied in some organs, I mean those that are instruments of food, like the stomach: for in it there is an attractor (jādhiba) for food from the mouth, a retainer (māsika) for it in it, a changer (mughayyira) to what is suitable to become blood in the liver, and an expeller (dāfi'a) for wastes to the intestines; then an attractor (jādhiba) for the blood that becomes food for the substance of the stomach like other organs, a retainer (māsika) for it until it changes to the similarity of the stomach's substance, a digestive (hāḍima) that does that, and an expeller (dāfi'a) for what mixes with it of the non-conforming. And similarly the liver and the veins.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage explains the statement (likely from the matn) that the four faculties exist in every organ and may be multiplied in some. The meaning is that every organ requires the attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive powers for its survival through nutrition. In some organs, especially those serving as instruments of food like the stomach, these powers are multiplied. The stomach has an attractor for food from the mouth, a retainer to hold it, a changer to prepare it to become blood in the liver, and an expeller for wastes. Additionally, it has a secondary set: an attractor for blood that becomes its own food, a retainer for that blood, a digestive to transform it, and an expeller for non-conforming elements. This multiplication reflects the stomach's central role. The same applies to the liver and veins.

Misconception: The four faculties are present only in certain major organs like the stomach and liver.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the four faculties exist in every organ because each organ requires nutrition. Multiplication, however, occurs only in some organs that serve as instruments of food.

Key Takeaway
Every organ possesses the four faculties for nutrition, and organs like the stomach may have multiple sets to handle both systemic and local nutrition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Your stomach expels not only waste but also what is not suitable from its own nourishment. What non-conforming elements—habits, thoughts, or relationships—are you allowing to mix with your spiritual intake? What will you expel starting today?

The Denial of Generative Powers: Can Simple Matter Produce Complex Life?
Al-Taftazani presents the argument for denying generative powers and explains its philosophical basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that denies generative powers on the grounds that complex actions cannot arise from a simple, insentient power, and how this argument ultimately points to direct divine causation.

Core Idea
Complex life cannot arise from simple, unconscious matter.

Al-Taftazani now presents a significant objection to the theory of generative powers. Some thinkers argued that the intricate structures of living beings could not possibly originate from a mere simple power lodged in homogeneous matter. This argument, referenced from Imam al-Razi and later philosophers, uses the very complexity of creation to deny intermediaries and affirm God's direct action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument is framed as a certainty ('the intellect is decisive').
Look for the key terms that define the nature of the power and matter.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the claim: complex actions cannot come from a simple, insentient power. Understand why this is used to deny generative powers.
The argument implies that such actions directly require a wise agent.
3
Examine the two theories of semen
Compare Aristotle's view (homogeneous parts) with Hippocrates' view (distinct parts but mixed). How do both support the idea that the substrate is uniform?
This uniformity supposedly cannot produce diverse organs.
4
Consider the theological implication
Even if generative powers are denied, the passage shows that complexity demands a Creator. That is the ultimate point.
Al-Taftazani is not endorsing the denial but using it to strengthen the case for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ونفاها بعضهم) : للقطع باستحالة صدور مثل هذه الأفعال التي هي العمدة في الاستدلال على قدرة الصانع وعلمه وحكمته عن قوة بسيطة عديمة الشعور، حالة في مادة متشابهة الأجزاء أو الالتصاق. إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام واختاره بعض الحكماء المتأخرين. وهو أن العقل قاطع بامتناع صدور هذه الأفعال المختلفة، والتركيبات العجيبة الدالة على غاية القدرة والحكمة على قوة بسيطة ليس لها شعور أصلا، مع أنها حالة في جسم متشابه الأجزاء، أو متشابه الامتزاج على اختلاف الرأيين، إذ عند أرسطو جزء المنى كالكل في الاسم والحد من غير اختلاف في الحقيقة لكونه منفصلاً عن الانثيين فقط، وعند أبقراط أجزاء المنى مختلفة بالحقائق، متمايزة في نفس الأمر، إذ يخرج من اللحم جزء شبيه به ومن العظم جزء شبيه به، وكذا سائر الأعضاء، غاية الأمر أنها غير متمايزة في الحس، وهو معنى تشابه الامتزاج، ولكل من الفريقين احتجاجات مذكورة في موضعها .
He said: (And some denied them) because of the certainty that such actions, which are the mainstay in inferring the power, knowledge, and wisdom of the Creator, cannot proceed from a simple, non-sentient power, existing in a material with similar parts or cohesion. This is a reference to what the Imam mentioned and adopted by some later philosophers. It is that the intellect is decisive that such different actions and wondrous compositions, which indicate the utmost power and wisdom, cannot proceed from a simple power that has no sentience at all, while it exists in a body with similar parts, or similar mixture according to the two opinions. For according to Aristotle, a part of the semen is like the whole in name and definition, with no difference in reality, because it is separated only from the two testicles. And according to Hippocrates, the parts of the semen are different in reality, distinct in themselves, for from flesh comes a part similar to it, from bone a part similar to it, and likewise the rest of the organs, except that they are not distinct in sensation, which is the meaning of similar mixture. And for each of the two groups there are proofs mentioned in their place.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the existence of the generative power and the response implied by the author. Some thinkers deny these powers (the three: generative, formative, articulating) on the grounds that it is impossible for such complex actions—which are the primary evidence for the Creator's power, knowledge, and wisdom—to originate from a simple, insentient power that resides in a homogeneous material. The author notes that this is a reference to what the Imam (likely al-Razi) mentioned and adopted by some later philosophers. The argument is that the intellect decisively knows that such diverse actions and marvelous compositions, indicative of supreme power and wisdom, cannot proceed from a simple power devoid of any consciousness, especially since it inheres in a body that is either uniform in parts (Aristotle's view) or uniform in mixture (Hippocrates' view). The passage then explains the two different ancient theories of semen: Aristotle holds that every part of the semen is identical in definition and nature, as it comes only from the testicles; Hippocrates holds that the parts of the semen are actually different in reality, each corresponding to the organ it will form, but they are not distinguishable by the senses, hence the mixture appears uniform. Each view has its own proofs. The implication is that if such simple matter cannot produce complex organs by itself, then there must be an external agent, i.e., God, and thus the denial of these powers is not necessarily problematic; rather, it reinforces divine causation.

Key Terms
القوة البسيطة
عديمة الشعور
تشابه الأجزاء
تشابه الامتزاج
أرسطو
أبقراط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers might think Al-Taftazani endorses the denial of generative powers fully.
Correction: In fact, he presents the argument to show that even if true, it leads to affirming a higher cause, strengthening the case for God's direct action.

Checkpoint

A) The powers are not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Complex actions cannot proceed from a simple, insentient power.
C) The powers are unnecessary for embryological development.
D) Aristotle and Hippocrates disagreed on the nature of semen.

Key Takeaway
The denial of generative powers does not negate divine causation; it actually emphasizes God's direct creative action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine the intricate design of your own body—your bones, flesh, and organs—do you truly believe that blind, unconscious matter could have produced this? What does that recognition demand of you right now? How must your relationship with your Creator change in light of this?
?
If you have ever attributed the wonders of nature to mere chance or natural processes, how does this argument challenge that assumption? Will you still take for granted the intricate design in a single cell, or will you see it as a sign that calls you to worship?
?
The philosophers disagreed on the nature of semen, but all agreed it cannot by itself produce life. You know that God is the Creator. Yet do you live as if your daily bread and health are purely natural? Are you relying on secondary causes as if they were independent? Confess one area where you have neglected to see God's direct hand.

Two Views on the Generative Power: Efficient or Inclusive?
Defining the second and third opinions on the generative power with reference to Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third definitions of the generative power in Islamic medicine and philosophy, and how they relate to the efficient, articulating, and formative powers.

Core Idea
The generative power is defined in two ways: as solely efficient or as inclusive of both efficient and articulating.

This brief passage presents two competing definitions of the generative power (al-muwallida) from classical Islamic thought. The second opinion restricts it to the efficient cause, while the third, favored by the majority, expands it to include both efficient and articulating functions. A citation from Ibn Sina's al-Qanun clarifies the subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the passage and locate the second and third opinions on the generative power. Notice how each is introduced.
The first view is not included in this passage; we start with the second.
2
Understand the second view
The second view restricts al-muwallida to al-muhassila (the efficient cause). It is attributed to some ancients and supported by a report from Ibn Sina.
Note the mention of two powers serving the generative: al-mufassila (articulating) and al-musawwira (formative).
3
Understand the third view
The third view makes al-muwallida a name covering both al-muhassila and al-mufassila. This is the majority view and is explicitly stated in al-Qanun.
This view broadens the scope of the generative power.
4
Examine the Qanun citation
Read the quotation from al-Qanun: it divides the preserving power into generative and formative, and subdivides generative into two types.
Notice the generative subtypes: one generates semen, the other mixes the powers appropriately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتفرد بالجبروت عز شأنه، والثاني: أعني كون المولدة للمحصلة، مذهب بعض الأقدمين، وبه يشعر ما نقل عن ابن سينا أن القوة المولدة يخدمها القوتان اللتان إحداهما المفصلة، والأخرى المصورة والثالث: أعني كونها اسماً لما يعم المحصلة والمفصلة. مذهب الجمهور والمصرح به في القانون حيث قال : إن القوة المتصرفة لبقاء النوع تنقسم إلى نوعين إلى المولدة والمصورة والمولدة نوعان نوع يولد المنى في الذكر والأنثى، ونوع يفصل القوى التي في المنى فيمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو.
The Unique in Majesty, exalted be His glory, and the second: I mean (the view that) the generative power is for the efficient cause, the doctrine of some ancients, and it is suggested by what was transmitted from Ibn Sina that the generative power is served by two powers, one of which is the articulating power and the other the formative power. And the third: I mean that it is a name for what encompasses the efficient and the articulating. This is the doctrine of the majority and what is stated explicitly in the Qanun where he said: The power that manages for the preservation of the species is divided into two types: the generative and the formative. The generative has two types: a type that generates semen in the male and female, and a type that separates the powers in the semen and mixes them into mixtures according to each organ.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the second and third opinions on the scope of the generative power (al-muwallida). The author first mentions the second opinion, which holds that the generative power is specifically for the efficient cause (al-muhassila). He notes that this view is held by some of the ancients and is indicated by a report from Ibn Sina that the generative power is served by two powers: the articulating (al-mufassila) and the formative (al-musawwira). The third opinion, which the author states is the majority view and explicitly mentioned in Ibn Sina's al-Qanun, is that the generative power is a name covering both the efficient and the articulating powers. The author then quotes al-Qanun, which divides the power that manages for the preservation of the species into generative and formative, and subdivides generative into two: one that generates semen in male and female, and one that separates and mixes the powers in the semen appropriately for each organ.

المحصلة
المفصلة
المصورة
القانون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'generative power' refers only to producing semen.
Correction: The third view, which is the majority, holds that generative power includes both efficient and articulating functions, not just semen production.

The generative power according to the majority opinion encompasses both the efficient cause and the articulating power, as stated in al-Qanun.
